<p>The <b>North Sea</b> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" title="Marginal sea">marginal sea</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> located between <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>. An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epeiric_sea" title="Epeiric sea">epeiric</a> (or "shelf") sea on the <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>, it connects to the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">ocean</a> through the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> in the south and the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a> in the north. It is more than 970 kilometres (600 mi) long and 580 kilometres (360 mi) wide, with an area of around 570,000 square kilometres (220,000 sq mi).</p>
<p>The North Sea has long been the site of important European shipping lanes as well as a major <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fishery</a>. The sea is a popular destination for recreation and tourism in bordering countries and more recently has developed into a rich source of energy resources including <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_energy" title="Wind energy">wind</a>, and early efforts in <a href="/wiki/Wave_power" title="Wave power">wave power</a>.</p>
<p>Historically, the North Sea has featured prominently in geopolitical and military affairs, particularly in Northern Europe. It was also important globally through the power northern Europeans projected worldwide during much of the Middle Ages and into the modern era. The North Sea was the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Vikings' rise</a>. Subsequently, the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Netherlands</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> each sought to dominate the North Sea and thus the access to the markets and resources of the world. As Germany's only outlet to the ocean, the North Sea continued to be strategically important through both World Wars.</p>
<p>The coast of the North Sea presents a diversity of geological and geographical features. In the north, deep <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> and sheer cliffs mark the <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scottish</a> coastlines, whereas in the south the coast consists primarily of sandy beaches and wide <a href="/wiki/Mudflat" title="Mudflat">mudflats</a>. Due to the dense population, heavy industrialization, and intense use of the sea and area surrounding it, there have been a number of environmental issues affecting the sea's ecosystems. Adverse environmental issues — commonly including <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>, industrial and agricultural <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">dredging</a>, and dumping among others — have led to a number of efforts to prevent degradation of the sea while still making use of its economic potential.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_the_North_Sea" title="Geography of the North Sea">Geography of the North Sea</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_discharging_into_the_North_Sea" title="List of rivers discharging into the North Sea">List of rivers discharging into the North Sea</a></div>
<div class="noresize thumb tright" style=";">
<div class="thumbinner" style="overflow:hidden;width:242px;">
<div class="thumbimage" style="height:315px; overflow:hidden; position:relative; background-color:white;">
<div style=";left:0px; top:0px; width:250px; position:absolute;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nseamap.gif" title="Sk=Skagerrak   Ka=KattegatEng Ch=English Channel"><img alt="" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="280" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Nseamap.gif/250px-Nseamap.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Nseamap.gif 1.5x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div style="text-align:left; background-color:transparent; line-height:110%;">
<div id="annotation_106x142" style="position:absolute; left:106px; top:142px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><b>North<br/>
Sea</b></span></div>
<div id="annotation_81x10" style="position:absolute; left:81px; top:10px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><b><a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian<br/>
Sea</a></b></span></div>
<div id="annotation_163x152" style="position:absolute; left:163px; top:152px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><b><a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Sk</a></b></span></div>
<div id="annotation_188x166" style="position:absolute; left:188px; top:166px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><b><a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Ka</a></b></span></div>
<div id="annotation_24x280" style="position:absolute; left:24px; top:280px; line-height:110%"><span style="background-color:transparent;"><b><a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">Eng Ch</a></b></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nseamap.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="374" data-file-width="280" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Nseamap.gif/250px-Nseamap.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Nseamap.gif 1.5x" width="250"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nseamap.gif" title="File:Nseamap.gif"></a></div>
Sk=<a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a>   Ka=<a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a><br/>
Eng Ch=<a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The North Sea is bounded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orkney_Islands" title="Orkney Islands">Orkney Islands</a> and east coast of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> to the west<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> and the northern and <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central European</a> mainland to the east and south, including <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-American-2">[2]</a></sup> In the southwest, beyond the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Dover" title="Strait of Dover">Straits of Dover</a>, the North Sea becomes the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> connecting to the Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-American-2">[2]</a></sup> In the east, it connects to the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> via the <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kattegat" title="Kattegat">Kattegat</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-American-2">[2]</a></sup> narrow straits that separate Denmark from Norway and Sweden respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> In the north it is bordered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shetland_Islands" title="Shetland Islands">Shetland Islands</a>, and connects with the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, which lies in the very north-eastern part of the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>The North Sea is more than 970 kilometres (600 mi) long and 580 kilometres (360 mi) wide, with an area of 570,000 square kilometres (220,000 sq mi) and a volume of 54,000 cubic kilometres (13,000 cu mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safety_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Safety-4">[4]</a></sup> Around the edges of the North Sea are <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_largest_islands_in_the_North_Sea" title="List of the largest islands in the North Sea">sizeable islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Frisian_Islands" title="Frisian Islands">Frisian Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-American_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-American-2">[2]</a></sup> The North Sea receives freshwater from a number of European continental watersheds, as well as the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>. A large part of the European <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a> empties into the North Sea, including water from the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>. The largest and most important rivers flowing into the North Sea are the <a href="/wiki/Elbe" title="Elbe">Elbe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meuse_(river)" title="Meuse (river)">Meuse</a> watershed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ray_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ray-5">[5]</a></sup> Around 185 million people live in the <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">catchment area</a> of <a href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_discharging_into_the_North_Sea" title="List of rivers discharging into the North Sea">the rivers discharging into the North Sea</a> encompassing some highly industrialized areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NorthSeaPDF_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-NorthSeaPDF-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_features">Major features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Major features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For the most part, the sea lies on the European <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a> with a mean depth of 90 metres (300 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calow_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calow-7">[7]</a></sup> The only exception is the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_trench" title="Norwegian trench">Norwegian trench</a>, which extends parallel to the Norwegian shoreline from <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a> to an area north of <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> It is between 20 and 30 kilometres (12 and 19 mi) wide and has a maximum depth of 725 metres (2,379 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lis_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lis-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank" title="Dogger Bank">Dogger Bank</a>, a vast <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraine</a>, or accumulation of unconsolidated glacial debris, rises to a mere 15 to 30 metres (50–100 ft) below the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostergren_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostergren-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> This feature has produced the finest fishing location of the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Long_Forties" title="Long Forties">Long Forties</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Broad_Fourteens" title="Broad Fourteens">Broad Fourteens</a> are large areas with roughly uniform depth in <a href="/wiki/Fathom" title="Fathom">fathoms</a>, (forty fathoms and fourteen fathoms or 73 and 26 m deep respectively). These great banks and others make the North Sea particularly hazardous to navigate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tuckey_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tuckey-11">[11]</a></sup> which has been alleviated by the implementation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_navigation_satellite_system" title="Global navigation satellite system">satellite navigation systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradford_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bradford-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Devil%27s_Hole_(North_Sea)" title="Devil's Hole (North Sea)">Devil's Hole</a> lies 200 miles (320 km) east of <a href="/wiki/Dundee" title="Dundee">Dundee</a>, Scotland. The feature is a series of asymmetrical trenches between 20 and 30 kilometres (12 and 19 mi) long, 1 and 2 kilometres (0.62 and 1.24 mi) wide and up to 230 metres (750 ft) deep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other areas which are less deep are <a href="/wiki/Cleaver_Bank" title="Cleaver Bank">Cleaver Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fisher_Bank" title="Fisher Bank">Fisher Bank</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noordhinder_Bank" title="Noordhinder Bank">Noordhinder Bank</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent">Extent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Extent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> defines the limits of the North Sea as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p><i>On the Southwest.</i> A line joining the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Walde_Lighthouse&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Walde Lighthouse (page does not exist)">Walde Lighthouse</a> (France, 1°55'E) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Leathercoat_Point&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Leathercoat Point (page does not exist)">Leathercoat Point</a> (England, 51°10'N).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>On the Northwest.</i> From <a href="/wiki/Dunnet_Head" title="Dunnet Head">Dunnet Head</a> (3°22'W) in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> to Tor Ness (58°47'N) in the Island of <a href="/wiki/Hoy" title="Hoy">Hoy</a>, thence through this island to the Kame of Hoy (58°55'N) on to Breck Ness on <a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Orkney" title="Mainland, Orkney">Mainland</a> (58°58'N) through this island to <a href="/wiki/Costa_Head" title="Costa Head">Costa Head</a> (3°14'W) and to Inga Ness (59'17'N) in <a href="/wiki/Westray" title="Westray">Westray</a> through Westray, to Bow Head, across to Mull Head (North point of <a href="/wiki/Papa_Westray" title="Papa Westray">Papa Westray</a>) and on to Seal Skerry (North point of <a href="/wiki/North_Ronaldsay" title="North Ronaldsay">North Ronaldsay</a>) and thence to <a href="/wiki/Horse_Holm" title="Horse Holm">Horse Island</a> (South point of the <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a> Islands).</p>
<p><i>On the North.</i> From the North point (Fethaland Point) of the <a href="/wiki/Mainland,_Shetland" title="Mainland, Shetland">Mainland</a> of the Shetland Islands, across to Graveland Ness (60°39'N) in the Island of <a href="/wiki/Yell,_Shetland" title="Yell, Shetland">Yell</a>, through Yell to Gloup Ness (1°04'W) and across to Spoo Ness (60°45'N) in <a href="/wiki/Unst" title="Unst">Unst</a> island, through Unst to <a href="/wiki/Hermaness" title="Hermaness">Herma Ness</a> (60°51'N), on to the SW point of the Rumblings and to <a href="/wiki/Muckle_Flugga" title="Muckle Flugga">Muckle Flugga</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=North_Sea&amp;params=60_51_N_0_53_W_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">60°51′N</span> <span class="longitude">0°53′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">60.850°N 0.883°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">60.850; -0.883</span></span></span></a></span>) all these being included in the North Sea area; thence up the meridian of 0°53' West to the <a href="/wiki/61st_parallel_north" title="61st parallel north">parallel of 61°00' North</a> and eastward along this parallel to the coast of Norway, the whole of Viking Bank being thus included in the North Sea.</p>
<i>On the East.</i> The Western limit of the <a href="/wiki/Skagerrak" title="Skagerrak">Skagerrak</a> [A line joining <a href="/wiki/Hanstholm" title="Hanstholm">Hanstholm</a> (<span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=North_Sea&amp;params=57_07_N_8_36_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">57°07′N</span> <span class="longitude">8°36′E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">57.117°N 8.600°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">57.117; 8.600</span></span></span></a></span>) and the Naze (<a href="/wiki/Lindesnes" title="Lindesnes">Lindesnes</a>, <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=North_Sea&amp;params=58_N_7_E_"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">58°N</span> <span class="longitude">7°E</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">58°N 7°E</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">58; 7</span></span></span></a></span>)].</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrology">Hydrology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hydrology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperature_and_salinity">Temperature and salinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Temperature and salinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The average temperature in summer is 17 °C (63 °F) and 6 °C (43 °F) in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safety_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Safety-4">[4]</a></sup> The average temperatures have been trending higher since 1988, which has been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Air temperatures in January range on average between 0 to 4 °C (32 to 39 °F) and in July between 13 to 18 °C (55 to 64 °F). The winter months see frequent gales and storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> averages between 34 to 35 grams of salt per litre of water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safety_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Safety-4">[4]</a></sup> The salinity has the highest variability where there is <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> inflow, such as at the Rhine and Elbe estuaries, the Baltic Sea exit and along the coast of Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reddy_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reddy-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_circulation_and_tides">Water circulation and tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Water circulation and tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The main pattern to the flow of water in the North Sea is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-clockwise" title="Anti-clockwise">anti-clockwise</a> rotation along the edges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The North Sea is an arm of the Atlantic Ocean receiving the majority of <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean current</a> from the northwest opening, and a lesser portion of warm current from the smaller opening at the English Channel. These tidal currents leave along the Norwegian coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SaS_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-SaS-20">[20]</a></sup> Surface and deep water currents may move in different directions. Low salinity surface coastal waters move offshore, and deeper, denser high salinity waters move in shore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The North Sea located on the continental shelf has different waves from those in deep ocean water. The wave speeds are diminished and the wave amplitudes are increased. In the North Sea there are two <a href="/wiki/Amphidromic_point" title="Amphidromic point">amphidromic</a> systems and a third incomplete amphidromic system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dyke_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dyke-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carter_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carter-23">[23]</a></sup> In the North Sea the average tide difference in wave amplitude is between 0 to 8 metres (0 to 26 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Safety_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Safety-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NorthSeaCurrents.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="1300" height="366" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/NorthSeaCurrents.png/280px-NorthSeaCurrents.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/NorthSeaCurrents.png/420px-NorthSeaCurrents.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/NorthSeaCurrents.png/560px-NorthSeaCurrents.png 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NorthSeaCurrents.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">Ocean currents</a> mainly entering via the north entrance exiting along Norwegian coast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Kelvin tide of the Atlantic ocean is a semidiurnal wave that travels northward. Some of the energy from this wave travels through the English Channel into the North Sea. The wave still travels northward in the Atlantic Ocean, and once past the northern tip of Great Britain, the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin_wave" title="Kelvin wave">Kelvin wave</a> turns east and south and once again enters into the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Changing_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Changing-24">[24]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coasts">Coasts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coasts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coastline_of_the_North_Sea" title="Coastline of the North Sea">Coastline of the North Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="803" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg/220px-Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg/330px-Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg/440px-Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wattenmeer-Nordfriesland.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The German North Sea coast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The eastern and western coasts of the North Sea are jagged, formed by <a href="/wiki/Glacier" title="Glacier">glaciers</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">ice ages</a>. The coastlines along the southernmost part are covered with the remains of deposited glacial sediment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> The Norwegian mountains plunge into the sea creating deep <a href="/wiki/Fjord" title="Fjord">fjords</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagos</a>. South of Stavanger, the coast softens, the islands become fewer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> The eastern Scottish coast is similar, though less severe than Norway. From <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">north east of England</a>, the cliffs become lower and are composed of less resistant <a href="/wiki/Moraine" title="Moraine">moraine</a>, which erodes more easily, so that the coasts have more rounded contours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EastRiding_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-EastRiding-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurosion_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eurosion-60">[60]</a></sup> In the Netherlands, Belgium and in <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Littoral" title="Littoral">littoral</a> is low and marshy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> The east coast and south-east of the North Sea (<a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea" title="Wadden Sea">Wadden Sea</a>) have coastlines that are mainly sandy and straight owing to <a href="/wiki/Longshore_drift" title="Longshore drift">longshore drift</a>, particularly along Belgium and Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHC_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-GHC-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_management">Coastal management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Coastal management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Afsluitdijk" title="Afsluitdijk">Afsluitdijk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delta_Works" title="Delta Works">Delta Works</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flood_control_in_the_Netherlands" title="Flood control in the Netherlands">Flood control in the Netherlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thames_Barrier" title="Thames Barrier">Thames Barrier</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zuiderzee_Works" title="Zuiderzee Works">Zuiderzee Works</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3310" data-file-width="4591" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg/220px-Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg/330px-Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg/440px-Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afsluitdijk_1031.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Afsluitdijk" title="Afsluitdijk">Afsluitdijk</a> (Closure-dike) is a major dam in the Netherlands</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The southern coastal areas were originally amphibious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flood_plain" title="Flood plain">flood plains</a> and swampy land. In areas especially vulnerable to storm surges, people settled behind elevated levees and on natural areas of high ground such as <a href="/wiki/Spit_(landform)" title="Spit (landform)">spits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geest" title="Geest">geestland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wefer_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wefer-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:[302,303]</sup> As early as 500 BC, people were constructing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_dwelling_hill" title="Artificial dwelling hill">artificial dwelling hills</a> higher than the prevailing flood levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wefer_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wefer-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:[306,308]</sup> It was only around the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, in 1200 AD, that inhabitants began to connect single ring dikes into a dike line along the entire coast, thereby turning amphibious regions between the land and the sea into permanent solid ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wefer_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wefer-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>The modern form of the dikes supplemented by overflow and lateral diversion channels, began to appear in the 17th and 18th centuries, built in the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The North Sea Floods of 1953 and 1962 were impetus for further raising of the dikes as well as the shortening of the coast line so as to present as little surface area as possible to the punishment of the sea and the storms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Currently, 27% of the Netherlands is below sea level protected by dikes, dunes, and beach flats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coastal_management" title="Coastal management">Coastal management</a> today consists of several levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BASF_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-BASF-66">[66]</a></sup> The dike slope reduces the energy of the incoming sea, so that the dike itself does not receive the full impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BASF_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-BASF-66">[66]</a></sup> Dikes that lie directly on the sea are especially reinforced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BASF_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-BASF-66">[66]</a></sup> The dikes have, over the years, been repeatedly raised, sometimes up to 9 metres (30 ft) and have been made flatter to better reduce wave erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Where the dunes are sufficient to protect the land behind them from the sea, these dunes are planted with beach grass (<i><a href="/wiki/Ammophila_arenaria" title="Ammophila arenaria">Ammophila arenaria</a></i>) to protect them from erosion by wind, water, and foot traffic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storm_tides">Storm tides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Storm tides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Storm_tides_of_the_North_Sea" title="Storm tides of the North Sea">Storm tides of the North Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2366" data-file-width="2943" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg/220px-Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg/330px-Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg/440px-Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Watersnoodramp_1953.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Zuid-Beveland" title="Zuid-Beveland">Zuid-Beveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1953" title="North Sea flood of 1953">North Sea flood of 1953</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">Storm surges</a> threaten, in particular, the coasts of the Netherlands, Belgium, Germany, and Denmark and low lying areas of eastern England particularly around <a href="/wiki/The_Wash" title="The Wash">The Wash</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Fens" title="The Fens">Fens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GHC_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-GHC-61">[61]</a></sup> Storm surges are caused by changes in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">barometric pressure</a> combined with strong wind created <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocean_surface_wave" title="Ocean surface wave">wave action</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first recorded storm tide flood was the <i>Julianenflut</i>, on 17 February 1164. In its wake the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jadebusen" title="Jadebusen">Jadebusen</a>, (a bay on the coast of Germany), began to form. A storm tide in 1228 is recorded to have killed more than 100,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morin_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morin-70">[70]</a></sup> In 1362, the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Marcellus%27_flood" title="Saint Marcellus' flood">Second Marcellus Flood</a>, also known as the <i>Grote Manndrenke</i>, hit the entire southern coast of the North Sea. Chronicles of the time again record more than 100,000 deaths as large parts of the coast were lost permanently to the sea, including the now legendary <a href="/wiki/Lost_city" title="Lost city">lost city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rungholt" title="Rungholt">Rungholt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Untergang_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Untergang-71">[71]</a></sup> In the 20th century, the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1953" title="North Sea flood of 1953">North Sea flood of 1953</a> flooded several nations' coasts and cost more than 2,000 lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> 315 citizens of Hamburg died in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_flood_of_1962" title="North Sea flood of 1962">North Sea flood of 1962</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamb_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lamb-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:[79,86]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tsunamis">Tsunamis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tsunamis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Though rare, the North Sea has been the site of a number of historically documented <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Storegga_Slide" title="Storegga Slide">Storegga Slides</a> were a series of underwater landslides, in which a piece of the Norwegian continental shelf slid into the Norwegian Sea. The immense landslips occurred between 8150 BCE and 6000 BCE, and caused a tsunami up to 20 metres (66 ft) high that swept through the North Sea, having the greatest effect on Scotland and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faeroe Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spiegel_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-spiegel-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1580_Dover_Straits_earthquake" title="1580 Dover Straits earthquake">Dover Straits earthquake of 1580</a> is among the first recorded earthquakes in the North Sea measuring between 5.6 and 5.9 on the Richter scale. This event caused extensive damage in <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a> both through its tremors and possibly triggered a <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>, though this has never been confirmed. The theory is a vast underwater landslide in the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a> was triggered by the earthquake, which in turn caused a tsunami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belgium_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-76">[76]</a></sup> The tsunami triggered by the <a href="/wiki/1755_Lisbon_earthquake" title="1755 Lisbon earthquake">1755 Lisbon earthquake</a> reached Holland, although the waves had lost their destructive power. The largest earthquake ever recorded in the United Kingdom was the <a href="/wiki/1931_Dogger_Bank_earthquake" title="1931 Dogger Bank earthquake">1931 Dogger Bank earthquake</a>, which measured 6.1 on the <a href="/wiki/Richter_magnitude_scale" title="Richter magnitude scale">Richter magnitude scale</a> and caused a small tsunami that flooded parts of the British coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Belgium_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Belgium-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_the_North_Sea" title="Geology of the North Sea">Geology of the North Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_southern_North_Sea" title="Geology of southern North Sea">Geology of southern North Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="747" data-file-width="1038" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg/220px-Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg/330px-Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg/440px-Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mediterranean_Rupelian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The North Sea between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=34">34</a> million years ago</span> and <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=28">28</a> million years ago</span>, as Central Europe became dry land</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shallow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epicontinental" title="Epicontinental">epicontinental</a> seas like the current North Sea have since long existed on the European <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">rifting</a> that formed the northern part of the Atlantic Ocean during the <a href="/wiki/Jurassic" title="Jurassic">Jurassic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> periods, from about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=150">150</a> million years ago</span>, caused <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">tectonic uplift</a> in the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ziegler_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ziegler-77">[77]</a></sup> Since then, a shallow sea has almost continuously existed between the uplands of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fennoscandian_Shield" title="Fennoscandian Shield">Fennoscandian Shield</a> and the British Isles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> This precursor of the current North Sea has grown and shrunk with the rise and fall of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eustacy" title="Eustacy">eustatic</a> sea level during geologic time. Sometimes it was connected with other shallow seas, such as the sea above the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">Paris Basin</a> to the south-west, the <a href="/wiki/Paratethys" title="Paratethys">Paratethys Sea</a> to the south-east, or the <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Torsvik_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Torsvik-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Doggerland.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="545" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/220px-Doggerland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/330px-Doggerland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Doggerland.svg/440px-Doggerland.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Doggerland.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing hypothetical extent of <a href="/wiki/Doggerland" title="Doggerland">Doggerland</a> (c. 8,000 BC), which provided a land bridge between Great Britain and continental Europe</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the Late Cretaceous, about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=85">85</a> million years ago</span>, all of modern mainland Europe except for Scandinavia was a scattering of islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glennie_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glennie-80">[80]</a></sup> By the Early <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a>, <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=34–28">34 to 28</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, the emergence of Western and Central Europe had almost completely separated the North Sea from the Tethys Ocean, which gradually shrank to become the Mediterranean as Southern Europe and South West Asia became dry land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> The North Sea was cut off from the English Channel by a narrow <a href="/wiki/Land_bridge" title="Land bridge">land bridge</a> until that was breached by at least two catastrophic floods between 450,000 and 180,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Since the start of the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary" title="Quaternary">Quaternary</a> period about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2.6">2.6</a> million years ago</span>, the eustatic sea level has fallen during each glacial period and then risen again. Every time the <a href="/wiki/Ice_sheet" title="Ice sheet">ice sheet</a> reached its greatest extent, the North Sea became almost completely dry. The present-day coastline formed after the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> when the sea began to flood the European continental shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plant_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plant-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006 a bone fragment was found while drilling for oil in the north sea. Analysis indicated that it was a <a href="/wiki/Plateosaurus" title="Plateosaurus">Plateosaurus</a> from 199 to 216 million years ago. This was the deepest dinosaur fossil ever found and the first find for Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_history">Natural history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Natural history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish_and_shellfish">Fish and shellfish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fish and shellfish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_fish_of_the_North_Sea" title="List of fish of the North Sea">List of fish of the North Sea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg/220px-Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg/330px-Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg/440px-Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bank_met_ondermeer_kokkels_mosselen_en_Japanse_oesters_in_de_Waddenzee_bij_Schiermonnikoog.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Pacific_oyster" title="Pacific oyster">Pacific oysters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_mussel" title="Blue mussel">blue mussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cockle_(bivalve)" title="Cockle (bivalve)">cockles</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea" title="Wadden Sea">Wadden Sea</a> in the Netherlands</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">Copepods</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> are plentiful in the North Sea. These tiny organisms are crucial elements of the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> supporting many species of fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecoserve_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ecoserve-86">[86]</a></sup> Over 230 species of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> live in the North Sea. <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">Cod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whiting_(fish)" title="Whiting (fish)">whiting</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saithe" title="Saithe">saithe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plaice" title="Plaice">plaice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sole_(fish)" title="Sole (fish)">sole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pouting_(fish)" title="Pouting (fish)">pouting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sprat" title="Sprat">sprat</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sandeel" title="Sandeel">sandeel</a> are all very common and are fished commercially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecoserve_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ecoserve-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup> Due to the various depths of the North Sea trenches and differences in salinity, temperature, and water movement, some fish such as blue-mouth redfish and <a href="/wiki/Rabbitfish" title="Rabbitfish">rabbitfish</a> reside only in small areas of the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">Crustaceans</a> are also commonly found throughout the sea. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norway_lobster" title="Norway lobster">Norway lobster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pandalus_borealis" title="Pandalus borealis">deep-water prawns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Crangon_crangon" title="Crangon crangon">brown shrimp</a> are all commercially fished, but other species of <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caridea" title="Caridea">shrimp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyster" title="Oyster">oyster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clam" title="Clam">clams</a> all live in the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecoserve_86-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ecoserve-86">[86]</a></sup> Recently non-indigenous species have become established including the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_oyster" title="Pacific oyster">Pacific oyster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_jackknife_clam" title="Atlantic jackknife clam">Atlantic jackknife clam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birds">Birds</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Birds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The coasts of the North Sea are home to <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">nature reserves</a> including the <a href="/wiki/Ythan_Estuary" title="Ythan Estuary">Ythan Estuary</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fowlsheugh" title="Fowlsheugh">Fowlsheugh</a> Nature Preserve, and <a href="/wiki/Farne_Islands" title="Farne Islands">Farne Islands</a> in the UK and the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea_National_Parks" title="Wadden Sea National Parks">Wadden Sea National Parks</a> in Denmark, Germany and the Netherlands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecoserve_86-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ecoserve-86">[86]</a></sup> These locations provide breeding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a> for dozens of bird species. Tens of millions of birds make use of the North Sea for breeding, feeding, or migratory stopovers every year. Populations of black legged kittiwakes, Atlantic puffins, <a href="/wiki/Northern_fulmar" title="Northern fulmar">northern fulmars</a>, and species of <a href="/wiki/Petrel" title="Petrel">petrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaduck" title="Seaduck">seaducks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loon" title="Loon">loons</a> (divers), <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gull" title="Gull">gulls</a>, <a href="/wiki/Auk" title="Auk">auks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tern" title="Tern">terns</a>, and many other seabirds make these coasts popular for <a href="/wiki/Birdwatching" title="Birdwatching">birdwatching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ecoserve_86-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ecoserve-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_mammals">Marine mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Marine mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1153" data-file-width="1679" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG/220px-Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG/330px-Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG/440px-Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bottlenose_dolphin_with_young.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A female <a href="/wiki/Bottlenose_dolphin" title="Bottlenose dolphin">bottlenose dolphin</a> with her young in <a href="/wiki/Moray_Firth" title="Moray Firth">Moray Firth</a>, Scotland</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The North Sea is also home to marine mammals. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_seal" title="Common seal">Common seals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Harbour_porpoise" title="Harbour porpoise">harbour porpoises</a> can be found along the coasts, at marine installations, and on islands. The very northern North Sea islands such as the Shetland Islands are occasionally home to a larger variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinnipeds" title="Pinnipeds">pinnipeds</a> including <a href="/wiki/Bearded_seal" title="Bearded seal">bearded</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harp_seal" title="Harp seal">harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hooded_seal" title="Hooded seal">hooded</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed seals</a>, and even <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> North Sea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans">cetaceans</a> include various <a href="/wiki/Porpoise" title="Porpoise">porpoise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whale</a> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg/220px-Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg/330px-Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg/440px-Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_Bloom_in_the_North_Sea.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">Phytoplankton</a> bloom in the North Sea</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plant species in the North Sea include species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrack_(science)" title="Wrack (science)">wrack</a>, among them <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bladder_wrack" title="Bladder wrack">bladder wrack</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knotted_wrack" title="Knotted wrack">knotted wrack</a>, and serrated wrack. <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">Algae</a>, macroalgal, and <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>, such as oarweed and laminaria hyperboria, and species of <a href="/wiki/Maerl" title="Maerl">maerl</a> are found as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zostera" title="Zostera">Eelgrass</a>, formerly common in the entirety of the Wadden Sea, was nearly wiped out in the 20th century by a disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Seagrass" title="Seagrass">sea grass</a> used to coat huge tracts of ocean floor, but have been damaged by trawling and dredging have diminished its habitat and prevented its return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Invasive <a href="/wiki/Sargassum_muticum" title="Sargassum muticum">Japanese seaweed</a> has spread along the shores of the sea clogging harbours and inlets and has become a nuisance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity_and_conservation">Biodiversity and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Biodiversity and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Due to the heavy human populations and high level of industrialization along its shores, the wildlife of the North Sea has suffered from pollution, overhunting, and overfishing. <a href="/wiki/Flamingo" title="Flamingo">Flamingos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelicans</a> were once found along the southern shores of the North Sea, but became extinct over the 2nd millennium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Walruses frequented the Orkney Islands through the mid-16th century, as both Sable Island and Orkney Islands lay within its normal range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">Gray whales</a> also resided in the North Sea but were driven to extinction in the Atlantic in the 17th century<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Other species have dramatically declined in population, though they are still found. <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_right_whale" title="North Atlantic right whale">North Atlantic right whales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shad" title="Shad">shad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Batoidea" title="Batoidea">rays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skate_(fish)" title="Skate (fish)">skates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, and other species were common in the North Sea until the 20th century, when numbers declined due to <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Other factors like the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-indigenous_species" title="Non-indigenous species">non-indigenous species</a>, industrial and agricultural <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dredging" title="Dredging">dredging</a>, human-induced <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>, construction on coastal breeding and feeding grounds, sand and gravel extraction, <a href="/wiki/Offshore_construction" title="Offshore construction">offshore construction</a>, and heavy shipping traffic have also contributed to the decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OSPAR_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-OSPAR-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The OSPAR commission manages the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OSPAR" title="OSPAR">OSPAR</a> convention to counteract the harmful effects of human activity on wildlife in the North Sea, preserve <a href="/wiki/Endangered_species" title="Endangered species">endangered species</a>, and provide environmental protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> All North Sea border states are signatories of the <a href="/wiki/MARPOL_73/78" title="MARPOL 73/78">MARPOL 73/78</a> Accords, which preserve the marine environment by preventing pollution from ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Germany, Denmark, and the Netherlands also have a trilateral agreement for the protection of the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea" title="Wadden Sea">Wadden Sea</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mudflat" title="Mudflat">mudflats</a>, which run along the coasts of the three countries on the southern edge of the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Whaling">Whaling</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Whaling">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Whaling was an important economic activity from the 9th until the 13th century for Flemish whalers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-102">[102]</a></sup> The medieval Flemish, Basque and Norwegian whalers who were replaced in the 16th century by Dutch, English, Danes and Germans, took massive numbers of whales and dolphins and nearly depleted the right whales. This activity likely led to the extinction of the Atlantic population of the once common <a href="/wiki/Gray_whale" title="Gray whale">gray whale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> By 1902 the whaling had ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-102">[102]</a></sup> After being absent for 300 years a single gray whale returned,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> it probably was the first of many more to find its way through the now ice-free <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>. Once 16-metre (50 ft) "fish" were taken in large quantities at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Seine" title="Seine">River Seine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-105">[105]</a></sup> Perhaps the gray whale will someday return to its former Seine estuary breeding grounds and to the feeding grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Wadden_Sea" title="Wadden Sea">Wadden Sea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_105-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-105">[105]</a></sup> where it will again roil the sediments and release its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> nutrients that will benefit the ecosystem.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_North_Sea" title="History of the North Sea">History of the North Sea</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4293" data-file-width="6000" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg/220px-Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg/330px-Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg/440px-Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prima_Europe_tabula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 1482 recreation of a map from Ptolemy's <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i> showing the "Oceanus Germanicus"</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="348" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png/220px-Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png/330px-Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_eclipse_1715May03_Halley_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse_of_May_3,_1715" title="Solar eclipse of May 3, 1715">solar eclipse</a> 1715 map showing <i>The German Sea</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Through history <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_North_Sea#Naming" title="History of the North Sea">various names</a> have been used for the North Sea. One of the earliest recorded names was <i>Septentrionalis Oceanus</i>, or "Northern Ocean," which was cited by Pliny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The name "North Sea" probably came into English, however, via the Dutch "Noordzee", who named it thus either in contrast with the <a href="/wiki/Zuiderzee" title="Zuiderzee">Zuiderzee</a> ("South Sea"), located south of <a href="/wiki/Frisia" title="Frisia">Frisia</a>, or because the sea is generally to the north of the Netherlands. Before the adoption of "North Sea," the names used in English were "German Sea" or "German Ocean", referred to the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> names "Mare Gemanicum" and "Oceanus Germanicus",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> and these persisted in use until the First World War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other common names in use for long periods were the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> terms "Mare Frisicum",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> as well as their <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> equivalents, "Frisian Sea".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Looijenga_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Looijenga-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>The modern names of the sea in local languages are: <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>: <span lang="da" xml:lang="da"><i>Nordsøen</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>: <span lang="nl" xml:lang="nl"><i>Noordzee</i></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Low_Saxon_language" title="Dutch Low Saxon language">Dutch Low Saxon</a>: <span lang="nds-NL" xml:lang="nds-NL"><i>Noordzee</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr" xml:lang="fr"><i>Mer du Nord</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">West Frisian</a>: <span lang="fry" xml:lang="fry"><i>Noardsee</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de" xml:lang="de"><i>Nordsee</i></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_German_language" title="Low German language">Low German</a>: <span lang="nds" xml:lang="nds"><i>Noordsee</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/North_Frisian_language" title="North Frisian language">Northern Frisian</a>: <i>Weestsiie</i> (literally meaning "West Sea"), <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>: <span lang="no" xml:lang="no"><i>Nordsjøen</i></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nynorsk_language" title="Nynorsk language">Nynorsk</a>: <span lang="nn" xml:lang="nn"><i>Nordsjøen</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a>: <span lang="sco" xml:lang="sco"><i>German Ocean</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>: <span lang="sv" xml:lang="sv"><i>Nordsjön</i></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic_language" title="Scottish Gaelic language">Scottish Gaelic</a>: <span lang="gd" xml:lang="gd"><i>An Cuan a Tuath</i></span>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Flemish_language" title="West Flemish language">West Flemish</a>: <span lang="vls" xml:lang="vls"><i>Nôordzêe</i></span> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeeuws" title="Zeeuws">Zeeuws</a>: <i>Noôrdzeê</i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The North Sea has provided waterway access for commerce and conquest. Many areas have access to the North Sea because of its long coastline and the European rivers that empty into it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> The British Isles had been protected from invasion by the North Sea waters<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> until the <a href="/wiki/Roman_conquest_of_Britain" title="Roman conquest of Britain">Roman conquest of Britain</a> in 43 CE. The Romans established organised ports, which increased shipping, and began sustained trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cuyvers_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cuyvers-111">[111]</a></sup> When the Romans <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">abandoned</a> Britain in 410, the Germanic <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saxons" title="Saxons">Saxons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jutes" title="Jutes">Jutes</a> began the next great migration across the North Sea during the <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>. They made successive invasions of the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a> began in 793 with the attack on <a href="/wiki/Lindisfarne" title="Lindisfarne">Lindisfarne</a>; for the next quarter-millennium the Vikings ruled the North Sea. In their superior <a href="/wiki/Longship" title="Longship">longships</a>, they raided, traded, and established colonies and outposts along the coasts of the sea. From the Middle Ages through the 15th century, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">northern European</a> coastal ports exported domestic goods, dyes, linen, salt, metal goods and wine. The Scandinavian and Baltic areas shipped grain, fish, naval necessities, and timber. In turn the North Sea countries imported high-grade cloths, spices, and fruits from the Mediterranean region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrtishIsles_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrtishIsles-113">[113]</a></sup> Commerce during this era was mainly conducted by maritime trade due to underdeveloped roadways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrtishIsles_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-BrtishIsles-113">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 13th century the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, though centred on the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, started to control most of the trade through important members and outposts on the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EuropeanNaval_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-EuropeanNaval-114">[114]</a></sup> The League lost its dominance in the 16th century, as neighbouring states took control of former <a href="/wiki/Trade_route#Hanseatic_trade" title="Trade route">Hanseatic</a> cities and outposts. Their internal conflict prevented effective cooperation and defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-115">[115]</a></sup> As the League lost control of its maritime cities, new <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> emerged that provided Europe with Asian, American, and African goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ripley_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ripley-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_sail">Age of sail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Age of sail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Willem_van_de_Velde_(II)_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1782" data-file-width="2500" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg/220px-Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg/330px-Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg/440px-Willem_van_de_Velde_%28II%29_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Willem_van_de_Velde_(II)_-_De_verovering_van_het_Engelse_admiraalschip_de_%27Royal_Prince%27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Painting of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Days%27_Battle" title="Four Days' Battle">Four Days' Battle</a> of 1666 by <a href="/wiki/Willem_van_de_Velde_the_Younger" title="Willem van de Velde the Younger">Willem van de Velde the Younger</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 17th century <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">Dutch Golden Age</a> during which Dutch <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cod" title="Cod">cod</a> and whale fisheries reached an all time high<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrtishIsles_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-BrtishIsles-113">[113]</a></sup> saw Dutch power at its zenith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PowerPlenty_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-PowerPlenty-119">[119]</a></sup> Important overseas colonies, a vast merchant marine, powerful navy and large profits made the Dutch the main challengers to an ambitious England. This rivalry led to the first three <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Dutch_Wars" title="Anglo-Dutch Wars">Anglo-Dutch Wars</a> between 1652 and 1673, which ended with Dutch victories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PowerPlenty_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-PowerPlenty-119">[119]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> the Dutch prince <a href="/wiki/William_III_of_England" title="William III of England">William</a> ascended to the English throne. With both countries united, commercial, military, and political power shifted from Amsterdam to London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacDonald_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacDonald-120">[120]</a></sup> The British did not face a challenge to their dominance of the North Sea until the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bluecher_sinkend.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2205" data-file-width="2913" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bluecher_sinkend.jpg/220px-Bluecher_sinkend.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bluecher_sinkend.jpg/330px-Bluecher_sinkend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Bluecher_sinkend.jpg/440px-Bluecher_sinkend.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bluecher_sinkend.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German cruiser <a href="/wiki/SMS_Bl%C3%BCcher" title="SMS Blücher">SMS <i>Blücher</i></a> sinks in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dogger_Bank_(1915)" title="Battle of Dogger Bank (1915)">Battle of Dogger Bank</a> on 25 January 1915.</div>
</div>
</div>
