<p>Tensions in the North Sea were again heightened in 1904 by the <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank_incident" title="Dogger Bank incident">Dogger Bank incident</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a>, several ships of the Russian Baltic Fleet, which was on its way to the Far East, mistook British fishing boats for Japanese ships and fired on them, and then upon each other, near the Dogger Bank, nearly causing Britain to enter the war on the side of Japan.</p>
<p>During the First World War, Great Britain's <a href="/wiki/Grand_Fleet" title="Grand Fleet">Grand Fleet</a> and Germany's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaiserliche_Marine" title="Kaiserliche Marine">Kaiserliche Marine</a> faced each other in the North Sea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halpern_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halpern-122">[122]</a></sup> which became the main <a href="/wiki/Naval_warfare_of_World_War_I#Theaters" title="Naval warfare of World War I">theatre of the war</a> for surface action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halpern_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Halpern-122">[122]</a></sup> Britain's larger fleet and <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Mine_Barrage" title="North Sea Mine Barrage">North Sea Mine Barrage</a> were able to establish an effective blockade for most of the war, which restricted the <a href="/wiki/Central_Powers" title="Central Powers">Central Powers</a>' access to many crucial resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker-123">[123]</a></sup> Major battles included the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Heligoland_Bight_(1914)" title="Battle of Heligoland Bight (1914)">Battle of Heligoland Bight</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Osborne_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne-124">[124]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dogger_Bank_(1915)" title="Battle of Dogger Bank (1915)">Battle of the Dogger Bank</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navies_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-navies-125">[125]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jutland" title="Battle of Jutland">Battle of Jutland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-navies_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-navies-125">[125]</a></sup> World War I also brought the first extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Submarine_warfare" title="Submarine warfare">submarine warfare</a>, and a number of submarine actions occurred in the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Second World War also saw action in the North Sea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frank_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frank-127">[127]</a></sup> though it was restricted more to aircraft reconnaissance, and action by fighter/bomber aircraft, submarines, and smaller vessels such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minesweeper_(ship)" title="Minesweeper (ship)">minesweepers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the aftermath of the war, hundreds of thousands of tons of chemical weapons were disposed of by being dumped in the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaffka_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaffka-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the war, the North Sea lost much of its military significance because it is bordered only by <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> member-states. However, it gained significant economic importance in the 1960s as the states around the North Sea began full-scale exploitation of its <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">oil and gas resources</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The North Sea continues to be an active trade route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_sea_eez.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1034" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/North_sea_eez.PNG/220px-North_sea_eez.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/North_sea_eez.PNG/330px-North_sea_eez.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/North_sea_eez.PNG/440px-North_sea_eez.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:North_sea_eez.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zones</a> in the North Sea</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_status">Political status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Political status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Countries that border the North Sea all claim the 12 nautical miles (22 km; 14 mi) of <a href="/wiki/Territorial_waters" title="Territorial waters">territorial waters</a>, within which they have exclusive fishing rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barry_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barry-132">[132]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Common_Fisheries_Policy" title="Common Fisheries Policy">Common Fisheries Policy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) exists to coordinate fishing rights and assist with disputes between EU states and the EU border state of Norway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the discovery of mineral resources in the North Sea, the <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Continental_Shelf" title="Convention on the Continental Shelf">Convention on the Continental Shelf</a> established country rights largely divided along the median line. The median line is defined as the line "every point of which is equidistant from the nearest points of the baselines from which the breadth of the territorial sea of each State is measured."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The ocean floor border between Germany, the Netherlands, and Denmark was only reapportioned after protracted negotiations and a judgement of the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barry_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barry-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oil_and_gas">Oil and gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Oil and gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_oil_and_gas_fields_of_the_North_Sea" title="List of oil and gas fields of the North Sea">List of oil and gas fields of the North Sea</a></div>
<p>As early as 1859, oil was discovered in onshore areas around the North Sea and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> as early as 1910.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glennie_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glennie-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StatfjordA(Jarvin1982).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="776" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/220px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/330px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg/440px-StatfjordA%28Jarvin1982%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StatfjordA(Jarvin1982).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oil platform Statfjord A with the flotel Polymarine</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Test drilling began in 1966 and then, in 1969, <a href="/wiki/Phillips_Petroleum_Company" title="Phillips Petroleum Company">Phillips Petroleum Company</a> discovered the <a href="/wiki/Ekofisk_oil_field" title="Ekofisk oil field">Ekofisk oil field</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ekofisk_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ekofisk-136">[136]</a></sup> distinguished by valuable, low-sulphur oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lohne_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lohne-137">[137]</a></sup> Commercial exploitation began in 1971 with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petroleum_tanker" title="Petroleum tanker">tankers</a> and, after 1975, by a <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">pipeline</a>, first to <a href="/wiki/Teesside" title="Teesside">Teesside</a>, England and then, after 1977, also to <a href="/wiki/Emden" title="Emden">Emden</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p>
<p>The exploitation of the North Sea <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves" title="Oil reserves">oil reserves</a> began just before the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a>, and the climb of international oil prices made the large investments needed for extraction much more attractive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the production costs are relatively high, the quality of the oil, the political stability of the region, and the proximity of important markets in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">western Europe</a> has made the North Sea an important oil producing region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lohne_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lohne-137">[137]</a></sup> The largest single humanitarian catastrophe in the North Sea <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_industry" title="Petroleum industry">oil industry</a> was the destruction of the offshore <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platform</a> <a href="/wiki/Piper_Alpha" title="Piper Alpha">Piper Alpha</a> in 1988 in which 167 people lost their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides the Ekofisk oil field, the <a href="/wiki/Statfjord_oil_field" title="Statfjord oil field">Statfjord oil field</a> is also notable as it was the cause of the first pipeline to span the <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_trench" title="Norwegian trench">Norwegian trench</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The largest <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_field" title="Natural gas field">natural gas field</a> in the North Sea, <a href="/wiki/Troll_gas_field" title="Troll gas field">Troll gas field</a>, lies in the Norwegian trench dropping over 300 metres (980 ft) requiring the construction of the enormous <a href="/wiki/Troll_A_platform" title="Troll A platform">Troll A platform</a> to access it.</p>
<p>The price of <a href="/wiki/Brent_Crude" title="Brent Crude">Brent Crude</a>, one of the first types of oil extracted from the North Sea, is used today as a standard price for comparison for <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">crude oil</a> from the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The North Sea contains western Europe's largest oil and natural gas reserves and is one of the world's key non-OPEC producing regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the UK sector of the North Sea, the oil industry invested £14.4 billion in 2013, and was on track to spend £13 billion in 2014. Industry body <a href="/wiki/Oil_%26_Gas_UK" title="Oil &amp; Gas UK">Oil &amp; Gas UK</a> put the decline down to rising costs, lower production, high tax rates, and less exploration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShellUK_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShellUK-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fishing">Fishing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Fishing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg/220px-Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg/330px-Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg/440px-Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fischkutterquerinhafen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A trawler in <a href="/wiki/Nordstrand,_Germany" title="Nordstrand, Germany">Nordstrand, Germany</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fishing_in_the_North_Sea" title="Fishing in the North Sea">Fishing in the North Sea</a></div>
<p>The North Sea is Europe's main fishery accounting for over 5% of international commercial fish caught.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-1">[1]</a></sup> Fishing in the North Sea is concentrated in the southern part of the coastal waters. The main method of fishing is <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In 1995, the total volume of fish and shellfish caught in the North Sea was approximately 3.5 million tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> Besides fish, it is estimated that one million tonnes of unmarketable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/By-catch" title="By-catch">by-catch</a> is caught and discarded each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>In recent decades, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> has left many fisheries unproductive, disturbing marine <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> dynamics and costing jobs in the <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">fishing industry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Herring, cod and plaice fisheries may soon face the same plight as mackerel fishing, which ceased in the 1970s due to overfishing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> The objective of the European Union <a href="/wiki/Common_Fisheries_Policy" title="Common Fisheries Policy">Common Fisheries Policy</a> is to minimize the environmental impact associated with resource use by reducing fish discards, increasing productivity of fisheries, stabilising markets of fisheries and fish processing, and supplying fish at reasonable prices for the consumer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mineral_resources">Mineral resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Mineral resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1202" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg/220px-Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg/330px-Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg/440px-Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amber_Bernstein_many_stones.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unpolished <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> stones, in varying hues</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to oil, gas, and fish, the states along the North Sea also take millions of cubic metres per year of <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">sand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gravel" title="Gravel">gravel</a> from the ocean floor. These are used for <a href="/wiki/Beach_nourishment" title="Beach nourishment">beach nourishment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">land reclamation</a> and construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phua_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phua-151">[151]</a></sup> Rolled pieces of <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> may be picked up on the east coast of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rice_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rice-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy">Renewable energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Renewable energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_the_European_Union" title="Renewable energy in the European Union">Renewable energy in the European Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_offshore_wind_farms_in_the_North_Sea" title="List of offshore wind farms in the North Sea">List of offshore wind farms in the North Sea</a></div>
<p>Due to the strong <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">prevailing winds</a>, and shallow water, countries on the North Sea, particularly Germany and Denmark, have used the shore for <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a> since the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> The North Sea is the home of one of the first large-scale <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Offshore_wind_farm" title="Offshore wind farm">offshore wind farms</a> in the world, <a href="/wiki/Horns_Rev" title="Horns Rev">Horns Rev</a> 1, completed in 2002. Since then many other <a href="/wiki/Wind_farm" title="Wind farm">wind farms</a> have been commissioned in the North Sea (and elsewhere). As of 2013 the 630 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megawatt" title="Megawatt">megawatt</a> (MW) <a href="/wiki/London_Array" title="London Array">London Array</a> is the largest offshore wind farm in the world, with the 504 (MW) <a href="/wiki/Greater_Gabbard_wind_farm" title="Greater Gabbard wind farm">Greater Gabbard wind farm</a> the second largest, followed by the 367 MW <a href="/wiki/Walney_Wind_Farm" title="Walney Wind Farm">Walney Wind Farm</a>. All are off the coast of the UK. These projects will be dwarfed by subsequent wind farms that are in the pipeline, including <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank_Wind_Farm" title="Dogger Bank Wind Farm">Dogger Bank</a> at 4,800 MW, Norfolk Bank (7,200 MW), and Irish Sea (4,200 MW). At the end of June 2013 total European combined offshore wind energy capacity was 6,040 MW. UK installed 513.5 MW offshore windpower in the first half year of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EWEA2013_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-EWEA2013-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>The expansion of offshore wind farms has met with some resistance. Concerns have included shipping collisions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_wind_power#Offshore" title="Environmental effects of wind power">environmental effects</a> on ocean ecology and wildlife such as fish and migratory birds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> however, these concerns were found to be negligible in a long-term study in Denmark released in 2006 and again in a UK government study in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> There are also concerns about reliability,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> and the rising costs of constructing and maintaining offshore wind farms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Despite these, development of North Sea wind power is continuing, with plans for additional wind farms off the coasts of Germany, the Netherlands, and the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> There have also been proposals for a <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Offshore_Grid" title="North Sea Offshore Grid">transnational power grid in the North Sea</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> to connect <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Wind_Power_Hub" title="North Sea Wind Power Hub">new offshore wind farms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hub_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-hub-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Energy production from <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal power</a> is still in a pre-commercial stage. The <a href="/wiki/European_Marine_Energy_Centre" title="European Marine Energy Centre">European Marine Energy Centre</a> has installed a wave testing system at Billia Croo on the Orkney mainland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> and a tidal power testing station on the nearby island of <a href="/wiki/Eday" title="Eday">Eday</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Since 2003, a prototype <a href="/wiki/Wave_Dragon" title="Wave Dragon">Wave Dragon</a> energy converter has been in operation at Nissum Bredning fjord of northern Denmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Tourism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2305" data-file-width="3233" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg/220px-Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg/330px-Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg/440px-Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Netherlands-Scheveningen-beach-1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The beach in <a href="/wiki/Scheveningen" title="Scheveningen">Scheveningen</a>, Netherlands in c. 1900</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The beaches and coastal waters of the North Sea are destinations for tourists. The Belgian, Dutch, German and Danish coasts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-169">[169]</a></sup> are developed for tourism. The North Sea coast of the United Kingdom has tourist destinations with beach resorts and golf courses.<br/>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Fife" title="Kingdom of Fife">Fife</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> is famous for its <a href="/wiki/Links_(golf)" title="Links (golf)">links</a> golf courses. The coastal City of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Andrews" title="St. Andrews">St. Andrews</a> being renowned as the "Home of Golf". The coast of <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">North East England</a> has several tourist towns such as <a href="/wiki/Scarborough,_North_Yorkshire" title="Scarborough, North Yorkshire">Scarborough</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bridlington" title="Bridlington">Bridlington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seahouses" title="Seahouses">Seahouses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Whitby" title="Whitby">Whitby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood%27s_Bay" title="Robin Hood's Bay">Robin Hood's Bay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seaton_Carew" title="Seaton Carew">Seaton Carew</a>. The coast of North East England has long sandy beaches and links golfing locations such as <a href="/wiki/Seaton_Carew_Golf_Club" title="Seaton Carew Golf Club">Seaton Carew Golf Club</a> and Goswick Golf Club.<br/>
The <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Trail" title="North Sea Trail">North Sea Trail</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Long-distance_trail" title="Long-distance trail">long-distance trail</a> linking seven countries around the North Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> Windsurfing and sailing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> are popular sports because of the strong winds. <a href="/wiki/Mudflat_hiking" title="Mudflat hiking">Mudflat hiking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">recreational fishing</a> and birdwatching<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hall_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hall-169">[169]</a></sup> are among other activities.</p>
<p>The climatic conditions on the North Sea coast have been claimed to be healthful. As early as the 19th century, travellers used their stays on the North Sea coast as curative and restorative vacations. The sea air, temperature, wind, water, and sunshine are counted among the beneficial conditions that are said to activate the body's defences, improve circulation, strengthen the immune system, and have healing effects on the skin and the respiratory system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_traffic">Marine traffic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Marine traffic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_Sea_ports" title="List of North Sea ports">List of North Sea ports</a></div>
<p>The North Sea is important for marine transport and its shipping lanes are among the busiest in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barry_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barry-132">[132]</a></sup> Major ports are located along its coasts: <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, the busiest port in Europe and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_ports_by_cargo_tonnage" title="List of busiest ports by cargo tonnage">fourth busiest port in the world by tonnage</a> as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=North_Sea&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">Antwerp</a> (was 16th) and <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> (was 27th), <a href="/wiki/Bremen" title="Bremen">Bremen</a>/<a href="/wiki/Bremerhaven" title="Bremerhaven">Bremerhaven</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Felixstowe" title="Port of Felixstowe">Felixstowe</a>, both in the top 30 busiest <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_container_ports" title="List of busiest container ports">container seaports</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Zeebrugge" title="Port of Zeebrugge">Port of Bruges-Zeebrugge</a>, Europe's leading <a href="/wiki/Roll-on/roll-off" title="Roll-on/roll-off">ro-ro</a> port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg/220px-ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg/330px-ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg/440px-ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ECT_waalhaven_bij_nacht.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>, Netherlands</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Fishing boats, service boats for offshore industries, sport and pleasure craft, and merchant ships to and from <a href="/wiki/List_of_North_Sea_ports" title="List of North Sea ports">North Sea ports</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ports_of_the_Baltic_Sea" title="Ports of the Baltic Sea">Baltic ports</a> must share routes on the North Sea. The Dover Strait alone sees more than 400 commercial vessels a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Because of this volume, navigation in the North Sea can be difficult in high traffic zones, so ports have established elaborate <a href="/wiki/Vessel_traffic_service" title="Vessel traffic service">vessel traffic services</a> to monitor and direct ships into and out of port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freestone_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freestone-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>The North Sea coasts are home to numerous canals and canal systems to facilitate traffic between and among rivers, artificial harbours, and the sea. The <a href="/wiki/Kiel_Canal" title="Kiel Canal">Kiel Canal</a>, connecting the North Sea with the Baltic Sea, is the most heavily used artificial seaway in the world reporting an average of 89 ships per day not including sporting boats and other small watercraft in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kiel_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kiel-178">[178]</a></sup> It saves an average of 250 nautical miles (460 km; 290 mi), instead of the voyage around the <a href="/wiki/Jutland" title="Jutland">Jutland</a> peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kiel2_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kiel2-179">[179]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Canal" title="North Sea Canal">North Sea Canal</a> connects <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> with the North Sea.</p>
